# 77 Levels

_161 messages · 22 participants · 2011-10 → 2011-12_

---

### 77 Levels

- **From:** "Bill Klein" <wmklein@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2011-10-23T21:59:09-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
There was a recent thread with a question abou 77-levels. The quesiton 
appeared to be from a "do my homework" person, but I have my own quesiton 
abut this.  (and this is NOT homework <G>)

At one time, I heard that SOME compilers actually did "different things" 
with 77-levels versus an 01-level with no subordiant items (i.e. an 
elmentary 01-level).  I think at least one used different "word boundaries" 
for 77-levels and another placed 77-level entries either in a different type 
of storage or all "contiguous" at the beginning of storage (regardless ow 
where they were defined in worksint-storage).

So my quesiton is,

  Does anyone know of any "currently supported" compilers that do anything 
different with a 77-level from an elementary 01-leve?  If so, what is the 
difference (and what is the compiler)?

My *guess* is that none of the currently supported compilers make any 
difference (at least by default), but this is only a guess.
```

#### ↳ Re: 77 Levels

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-10-24T18:05:39+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9gka15F93qU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com>`

```
Bill Klein wrote:
> There was a recent thread with a question abou 77-levels. The quesiton
> appeared to be from a "do my homework" person, but I have my own
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> difference (at least by default), but this is only a guess.

The major difference (40 years ago) was the alignment of  items in storage. 
In the days when storage was expensive and in short supply, you could save 
bytes by usng 77 levels because they were allocated directly into storage 
without regard for any kind of boundary.

Different acrhitectures could perform better if they didn't have to modify 
addresses to align storage and compilers could save storage if they didn't 
have to allocate "slack" bytes to force internal alignment  (to a "word 
boundary") of stored items.

But of all of this has been irrelevant for decades now.

These days the use of a 77 level is just plain pointless and unnecessary. It 
has NO advantage over a 77 level, and it simply confuses people having to 
deal with COBOL legacy.

The ONLY use I would see for it would be for compatibility with ancient 
code.

Even if there was some esoteric COBOL compiler, running on some weird 
hardware that no-one's ever heard of, where boundary alignment actually 
improves performance, the AMOUNT of performance improvement is likely to be 
so slight that it would make no discernible difference.

I'm sticking with 01 levels...

Pete.

Use 01 and
```

```
On Mon, 24 Oct 2011 03:35:21 -0700 (PDT), Alistair Maclean
<alistair.j.l.maclean@gmail.com> wrote:

>On Oct 24, 4:59ï¿½am, "Bill Klein" <wmkl...@noreply.ix.netcom.com>
>wrote:
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>supplementary - does any one 77 levels?

In our mainframe application programs, we do make use of 77 levels
though not to the extent that we used to in days gone by.  The trend
now is to have maybe a couple of flags as 77 levels but to define most
other fields under various 01 level identifiers.  Like 01
WS-Working-Fields.  Then 05 or whatever you wish (our standard is 05)
fields under it usually prefixed by the 01 name.

For our clients that use Panvalet as a source library management, it
will add a 77 level item to all COBOL programs indicating module name,
level number, compile date and time.

But as I said, we do not utilize 77 levels as much as we used to.

Regards,
```

- **From:** Nomen Nescio <nobody@dizum.com>
- **Date:** 2011-10-24T14:27:29+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c0aa4e035904e6f53ff2ef63a1c8f36b@dizum.com>`

```
> At one time, I heard that SOME compilers actually did "different things" 
> with 77-levels versus an 01-level with no subordiant items (i.e. an 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> (regardless ow where they were defined in worksint-storage).

An 01 was always defined by IBM to be on a doubleword boundary. I believe
this is still the case but I don't have a z/OS system to test. 77 items were
aligned based on the data item defined.

>   Does anyone know of any "currently supported" compilers that do anything> different with a 77-level from an elementary 01-leve?  If so, what is the 
> difference (and what is the compiler)?

I would bet IBM has not changed this behavior and it is still relevant,
especially when processing tapes or when accessing storage in the LINKAGE
SECTION.

I disagree with what Pete says if I understand him to mean 77 should not be
used. Not every item is part of a structure or record, this should be very
obvious. There are elementary data items and they should be defined as
77. If that's not what you meant Pete then apologies.
```

```
I was always taught to use 77s for single line data items, and to use
01, 02 and 03 etc. for structures.  This has worked for me on various
minicomputer compilers (NCR, TI, RM-COS) and on RM-COBOL, Acucobol and
isCOBOL.  Pete is probably right that there's no need to use 77s any
longer, but , there's also no compelling reason not to.
```

```
Mattc wrote:
> I was always taught to use 77s for single line data items, and to use
> 01, 02 and 03 etc. for structures.  This has worked for me on various
> minicomputer compilers (NCR, TI, RM-COS) and on RM-COBOL, Acucobol and

Absolutely.

People should use whatever they feel comfortable with and whatever meets 
their particular priorities.

One of my priorities is to keep the code I write as consistent as possible 
so I tend not to mix alternative constructs.

The point of my comment was not that people shouldn't use 77, just that 
there is no real advantage in doing so any more.

I would strongly argue that people shouldn't use OCCURS ...DEPENDING ON, for 
instance, but not that they shouldn't use 77s :-)

Pete.
```

```
Arnold Trembley wrote:
> On 10/24/2011 6:30 PM, Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> Mattc wrote:
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
> absolutely necessary in those cases.

Not only unnecessary, but pointless. The compiler allocates the maximum 
storage anyway; it isn't dynamic storage as in other languages.

There MAY be a case that it can shorten the time taken for table entry 
compare. But by the time it has worked out the length and gone to registers 
a storage based compare would have succeeded anyway... (Unless the length of 
each table entry is VERY long... for normal purposes there is no real gain.)

I don't avoid it because I don't understand it; I avoid it because I DO 
understand it :-)

>
> I have used 77 levels on occasion, but still see them in older
> programs.

Yes, of course.

Pete.
```

```
On Oct 26, 1:15 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Arnold Trembley wrote:
> > On 10/24/2011 6:30 PM, Pete Dashwood wrote:
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
> storage anyway; it isn't dynamic storage as in other languages.

Dynamic storage can be very expensive in processing time. In general
there is a heap which is a fixed size initially from which storage can
be allocated or freed. When it becomes full or too fragmented another
large block is requested from the OS.

If a dynamic table is often added to or reduced then there may be
frequent expensive calls to alloc(), realloc() and free() or their
equivalents.

While ODO may actually use a fixed size table this has trivial
overheads compared to dynamic tables.

> There MAY be a case that it can shorten the time taken for table entry
> compare. But by the time it has worked out the length and gone to registers
> a storage based compare would have succeeded anyway... (Unless the length of
> each table entry is VERY long... for normal purposes there is no real gain.)

Is this just more uninformed dogma ?

> I don't avoid it because I don't understand it; I avoid it because I DO
> understand it :-)

You may understand the semantics but you seem to think that the
implementation of dynamic tables is magic.
```

```
>Arnold Trembley wrote:
>> On 10/24/2011 6:30 PM, Pete Dashwood wrote:

>each table entry is VERY long... for normal purposes there is no real gain.)

I believe this is the only way to indicate to SEARCH ALL the number of
valid entries when dealing with a number of entries that can vary
between executions (data from external sources, etc.).  If the unused
slots aren't initialized to maximum values interesting results could
occur.  They might also occur if less than half of the maximum number
of possible entries are loaded.

Clark Morris
```

```
On Oct 27, 1:13 pm, Clark F Morris <cfmpub...@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote:
> On Wed, 26 Oct 2011 14:10:13 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
>
…[43 more quoted lines elided]…
> known, how would you do the table look up.

I would look up an ISAM file. But then my systems have a general
purpose 'decode' file that is used for this sort of thing all the
time, so it is always available and easy to add different things to.

In an interactive session if a new entry is required it can be added
(using a different session) and then used without reloading the
program to reload the table.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Variable length tables was Re: 77 Levels

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>

```
Clark F Morris wrote:
>
> So assuming you were writing something in COBOL where the number of
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I wouldn't.

I'd write the data to a ISAM (or similar) file instead of a table. 
Thereafter, use file access to find what I wanted.

Before you blanch, with modern compilers, and the operating system's 
optimization of memory, I'd be really surprised if my suggestion wasn't 
faster than a binary search in RAM!

This file-method has the following benefits (in addition to speed)
* The executable module is smaller
* You need never worry - as a practical matter - about how large the table 
may get
* You don't have to kludge around COBOL's table size limitations
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ ODO with Intrinsic Functions (was Re: 77 Levels)

- **From:** Rick Smith <rs847925@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-11-02T12:25:40-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<33503ae4-31d5-4e81-9bd4-68ac2e08753c@n18g2000vbv.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com> <a35c4935-d3b0-4730-9c2c-cbc823e9774d@h30g2000pro.googlegroups.com> <9gmaolFmhU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Oct 24, 6:30 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> I would strongly argue that people shouldn't use OCCURS ...DEPENDING ON, for
> instance, but not that they shouldn't use 77s :-)

ISO/IEC 1989:2002
15.2 Arguments (Intrinsic functions)
"When the definition of a function permits an argument
to be repeated a variable number of times, a table may
be referenced by specifying the data-name and any
qualifiers that identify the table, followed immediately
by subscripting where one or more of the subscripts is
the word ALL.

"When ALL is specified as a subscript, the effect is as
if each table element associated with that subscript
position were specified. ... If the ALL subscript is
associated with a data item described with an OCCURS
DEPENDING ON clause, the range of values is determined
by the object of the OCCURS DEPENDING ON clause."

Intrinsic functions that can accept ALL table elements as
arguments (2002 and ANSI X3.23a-1989): MAX, MEAN, MEDIAN,
MIDRANGE, MIN, ORD-MAX, ORD-MIN, PRESENT-VALUE, RANGE,
STANDARD-DEVIATION, SUM, and VARIANCE.

For those functions, SEARCH, SEARCH ALL, and SORT (2002);
OCCURS DEPENDING ON simplifies the programming task.
It follows that, where a table may contain a variable
number of elements, the use of OCCURS DEPENDING ON should
be encouraged.
```

```
  With the exception of the table SORT featrue, I can't believe that ANY of 
the compilers that you have access to don't "correctly" handle ODOs for
 - All subscript
 - SEARCH ALL

I certainly know that Micro Focus, Fujitsu and IBM (mainframe and iSeriec) 
all CORRECTLY handle (use the current number of elments) for ODO's for these 
constructs.

Now, you still may not want to use ODO's as they do NOT save storage and 
they do (sometimes) add performance issues, but they do provide a "pgrammer 
function" that is not (easily) handled otherwise.  (the use of low-value 
entries at the begging or high-value at the end of xied length tables, works 
with SEARCH ALL but does NOT work with the "ALL" subscriptfor most intrinsic 
funcitons)
```

```
Mattc wrote:
> I was always taught to use 77s for single line data items, and to use
> 01, 02 and 03 etc. for structures.  This has worked for me on various
> minicomputer compilers (NCR, TI, RM-COS) and on RM-COBOL, Acucobol and
> isCOBOL.  Pete is probably right that there's no need to use 77s any
> longer, but , there's also no compelling reason not to.

But 77 levels do not easily lend themselves to GLOBAL or EXTERNAL 
directives. I mean, you COULD use them, but it's a lot of trouble. Much 
better in my view:

01  MISC-THINGS GLOBAL.
  02  A-THING...
  02  BIGGER-THING...
  02  COMPRESSED-THING...
  02  DARNED-THINGS.
      05  DARNED-THING-1...
      05  DARNED-THING-2...
  02  EDIBLE-THING...
  --- etc ---

I know, I know, but I LIKE to alphabetize working storage.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 77 Levels

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Mon, 21 Nov 2011 14:13:42 -0800 (PST), "john@wexfordpress.com"
> <john@wexfordpress.com> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> stored next to that table, and never used for any other table.

Yes, I do that too, inasmuch as I always use INDEXED BY and provide the 
required index as a unique index used ONLY by THAT table.


Most of my programs have a little definition in WORKING-STORAGE as follows:

01   subscripts usage comp-5.
      12 J                  pic s9(4) value zero.
      12 K                pic s9(4) value zero.
      12 L                 pic s9(4) value zero.
      12 M                pic s9(4) value zero.
      12 N                pic s9(4) value zero.

These work as general purpose registers and get used for things like 
intermediate calculations in string manipulation and reference modification.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: 77 Levels

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-11-23T14:50:34+13:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9j31rcFn3rU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com> <7d20edb2-dc60-438b-b099-169ac73ddc3e@o13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <o0qlc7ptg77js09mciqesjs7ooee7odc7q@4ax.com> <9j0c8sFsj7U1@mid.individual.net> <jaf2h4$ejf$1@reader1.panix.com> <o6lnc71uumfueqvoj440tp2925l94cpvgn@4ax.com>`

```
SkippyPB wrote:
> On Tue, 22 Nov 2011 02:48:04 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:
>
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> is a whole number.

comp-5 is little endian, as per x86 archtecture. You could just as easily 
make it comp, which IS valid in IBM COBOL.
>
> And Id be fired if I used subscripts with names like that!!

I'm sorry to hear that. I suggest that that would be their loss... :-) Hard 
to imagine that programmers couldn't deal with it, or that Site Management 
would be so rigid in adherence to a concept like "readability", when what 
they really mean is "verbosity". The letters are no harder to read than 
using blah-blah-sub. I have worked on sites where it was the site standard 
for subscript use in COBOL.

The point about them is that they have no fixed use (unlike an INDEX defined 
for a specific table), so you know that you cannot just expect them to 
contain the "right" value; they must be initialised before each use. (I use 
them a lot with PERFORM... VARYING and that automatically sets an initial 
value. I also find them useful for STRING and UNSTRING and INSPECT as 
Pointers and tallies. In fact just about anywhere you need an integer...). 
As soon as you see one in my code you KNOW it is NOT attached to any 
particular array or table and is just a local count or pointer for the 
construct where it is being used.

Please understand, I'm not advocating everyone should do this, I just 
mentioned it in passing. It is something I found useful during the decades 
when I wrote COBOL for a living. (And I first did it on IBM mainframes.)

Pete.
```

- **References:** `<vo5pq.3$1x3.0@en-nntp-16.dc1.easynews.com> <7d20edb2-dc60-438b-b099-169ac73ddc3e@o13g2000vbo.googlegroups.com> <o0qlc7ptg77js09mciqesjs7ooee7odc7q@4ax.com> <9j0c8sFsj7U1@mid.individual.net> <jaf2h4$ejf$1@reader1.panix.com> <o6lnc71uumfueqvoj440tp2925l94cpvgn@4ax.com> <9j31rcFn3rU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Wed, 23 Nov 2011 14:50:34 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>SkippyPB wrote:
>> On Tue, 22 Nov 2011 02:48:04 +0000 (UTC), docdwarf@panix.com () wrote:
…[47 more quoted lines elided]…
>when I wrote COBOL for a living. (And I first did it on IBM mainframes.)


I'd have to say that using the one character subscript/counter names
is a bad idea in Cobol.  They make absolute sense, and *add* to
clarity in more modular languages with local variables*.  A locally
scoped variable named, i, j, k, etc. in those languages is
idiomatically recognized as a a loop counter/subscript, with very
local usage and without the visual clutter of long variable names.

But it's bad if those have global scope, since they are no longer
contained, and have the same problems any variables with global scope
have.  Just consider the issues of one routine with a loop calling a
subroutine with a loop - you had darn well better not use i as a loop
counter in both.

The lack of proper subroutines (pretty much you need parameters and
local variables) is one of Cobol's greatest failings, and while it
more or less has them now, it took four decades, despite it being
perfectly clear that Cobol was horribly deficient in that area.
Basically original Fortran came close, and languages like Algol had
the whole boat no later than the mid-sixties.

As to indexes, those are another Cobol mistake.  Indexes made (some)
sense in the context of dumb compilers, much like the old Fortran
subscripting rules**, although the Fortran limitations were fairly
quickly eased.


*admittedly OO Cobol goes a fair way to providing that, and in that
case I have no objections, although that's certainly not anything like
typical Cobol usage

**Originally subscripts in Fortran were limited to the general form
((A*B) +/- C), and the obvious degenerate forms of that.  The point
was that those would be easy forms for the compiler to generate
efficient code.  Obviously that was quickly expanded to approximately
"any expression resulting in an integer."  Note that Fortran never had
a construct like indexes for performance reasons, even though the
application domain for Fortran made *much* heavier use of arrays than
Cobol applications did.
```

```
I completely agree with that. Peter's 'no fixed use' of global
iterators is poor practice. If there is a PERFORM loop using one of
the variables and within that there is a PERFORM paragraph then there
is nothing that will detect or prevent re-use of the variable at some
point.


> The lack of proper subroutines (pretty much you need parameters and
> local variables) is one of Cobol's greatest failings,

Complete nonsense. CALL / EXIT PROGRAM has been in COBOL since '74 and
was implemented by many vendors in slightly different ways in the 60s.
In ICL 1900 COBOL it was done with "ENTER language NAME" which would
call COBOL, FORTRAN or PLAN routines with parameters. The '74 compiler
used standard CALL.

Nested subroutines were in '85 COBOL, maybe you never got the memo.

   a)   "for performance reasons FORTRAN never had indexes"
or b)   "Fortran never had ( indexes for performance )"

In any case FORTRAN only had arrays of numbers (int, real, double,
etc) and abused them by storing text strings across array elements.
```

```
On Wed, 23 Nov 2011 09:48:49 -0800 (PST), Richard
<riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Nov 23, 10:59ï¿½pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>Nested subroutines were in '85 COBOL, maybe you never got the memo.


Subprograms, nested or otherwise, were largely unusable as general
subroutines.  Sure nested subprograms were better, but did you ever
see anyone write a program with no performs, just calls to nested
subprograms?  Obviously possible, but not something most people wopuld
have considered practical.  The lack of typing and a very expensive
call mechanism didn't help either.  And while not a direct fault of
the language, by the time Cobol-85 features became available, much of
the Cobol community had ossified into its final "we've always done
things this way" form.

And yes I got the memo, and got my hand smacked for using "weird stuff
like that."


>> and while it
>> more or less has them now, it took four decades, despite it being
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>Please explain why they were a "mistake".


Indexes added nothing to the expressiveness of than language (some
minor application to SEARCH aside), added considerable complexity to
use (this variable I can modify with an ADD, but *that* I need to use
SET on, and if I want to use the same index value on two arrays, I
have to maintain two separate indexes), and provided only a modest
amount of optimization support for the compiler anyway.

Most decent compilers are quite capable of optimizing most of the
subscripting to be equivalent of what you'd hope a index would
generate, without putting the additional burden on the programmer.  My
point with the Fortran example was that if the Fortran guys (or
frankly anyone else in programming language history) didn't find any
use for such a construct, and the Fortran guys were much more into
processing arrays than the Cobol community, and much bigger on CPU
performance (Cobol users typically did much more I/O, and while CPU
performance was not unimportant, it was less of a focus than for the
Fortran community).

Honestly, for a language intended to provide clarity to programmers
above all else, exposing a trivial micro-optimization like indexes is
just bizarre.


>> **Originally subscripts in Fortran were limited to the general form
>> ((A*B) +/- C), and the obvious degenerate forms of that. ï¿½The point
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>or b)   "Fortran never had ( indexes for performance )"


The Fortran community never implemented a mechanism such as indexes,
despite being considerably more interested in array access performance
than the Cobol folks.  Basic subscripting (with some initial
restrictions on the form of the subscript expression*), and elementary
optimization techniques, were good enough, and didn't require mucking
up the language.

Some languages that support pointers allow you to produce roughly
equivalent code (incrementing a pointer in C is much like "SET
INDEXNAME UP BY 1" in what it implies for the code), but pointers
wander into a lot of different areas as well.


*Cobol of course having a far greater restrictions on subscript/index
expressions.


>In any case FORTRAN only had arrays of numbers (int, real, double,
>etc) and abused them by storing text strings across array elements.


Yes, structures were not in original Fortran, although they were added
soon enough.  But that has only limited impact on the subscripting
problem.
```

```
On Nov 24, 10:02 am, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:

No, wrong. They were perfectly usable. It may well be that you did not

> Sure nested subprograms were better, but did you ever
> see anyone write a program with no performs, just calls to nested
> subprograms?  Obviously possible, but not something most people wopuld
> have considered practical.

Where is the requirement that they stop using PERFORMs in order to use
CALLs or vice versa? Both can be used in the same program. Or do you
still use GO TO for program structure ?

> The lack of typing and a very expensive
> call mechanism didn't help either.

COBOL can use dynamic CALLs or it can use static linked CALLs. Nested
program CALLs may well be less expensive than external CALLs but these
are not significantly more costly than similar features in other
languages.


> And while not a direct fault of
> the language, by the time Cobol-85 features became available, much of
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> like that."

Your original point was "COBOL's failings", now we find that they are
in fact programmer's failings, or programmer management failings.


> >> and while it
> >> more or less has them now, it took four decades, despite it being
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> amount of optimization support for the compiler anyway.

Indexes did not remove subscripts. If you found them confusing then
you had the option of not using them.

They did, in fact, generally improve efficiency, and provide better
error detection. They were not a "mistake", although it seems there
was a 'mistake' in expecting ossified programmers to drag themselves
out of the 60s.

If you didn't find a better performance on array access intensive
programs then you were probably doing it wrong. One commenter wrote:

"""| using binary data items (COMP) to address a table is 30% slower
than using indexes
| using packed decimal data items (COMP-3) to address a table is 300%
slower than using indexes
| using DISPLAY data items to address a table is 450% slower than
using indexes"""


> Most decent compilers are quite capable of optimizing most of the
> subscripting to be equivalent of what you'd hope a index would
> generate, without putting the additional burden on the programmer.

That still does not make index a "mistake".

> My
> point with the Fortran example was that if the Fortran guys (or
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> just bizarre.

Programmers are supposed to make informed decisions about which
constructs to use in particular situations. Indexes are not a 'trivial
micro-optimization', they are also a mechanism for ensuring
correctness by eliminating common programming faults. But it seems
that you never actually discovered where they could be beneficial.

Also I do not see that they reduce clarity, in fact they can enhance
it.


> >> **Originally subscripts in Fortran were limited to the general form
> >> ((A*B) +/- C), and the obvious degenerate forms of that.  The point
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> up the language.

Because FORTRAN originally had only a limited set of array element
sizes.

> Some languages that support pointers allow you to produce roughly
> equivalent code (incrementing a pointer in C is much like "SET
> INDEXNAME UP BY 1" in what it implies for the code), but pointers
> wander into a lot of different areas as well.

Depending on implemntation of course, yes a 'SET index UP BY 1' could
be equivalent to 'sptr += sizeof(s)' or indeed 'sptr++'. C programmers
find that is quite clear and use it because it is more efficient.

But if you get confused then don't use it, it may have been a
"mistake" to remove subscripting but they didn't. They added more
modern features.


> *Cobol of course having a far greater restrictions on subscript/index
> expressions.

Which is a _good_ thing. C programmers are known to abuse pointers and
gave them a bad name (which is why many modern languages don't have
explicit pointers). COBOL avoided allowing abuse. That was not a
"mistake".


> >In any case FORTRAN only had arrays of numbers (int, real, double,
> >etc) and abused them by storing text strings across array elements.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> problem.

"Soon enough" was Fortran90. More than a quarter century after the
original. Perhaps that is how long ossified programmers take to get
their head around a new idea.
```

```
On Wed, 23 Nov 2011 16:59:00 -0800 (PST), Richard

>
>>ï¿½Sure nested subprograms were better, but did you ever
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…

And it (call mechanism) is hardly "expensive".  It translates in the
Assembler to the standard calling mechanism between programs.  I.E.,
saved area address in register 13, parameter start address in register
1, subroutine address in register 14 and next instruction in calling
program address in register 15.  That standard has been in place for
many, many moons.  I'd say it has paid for itself long ago.

>
>> And while not a direct fault of
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…

…[21 more quoted lines elided]…

My thoughts exactly.  Yes you have to use the SET verb or increment or
decrement a field defined by INDEXED BY.  However, you don't have to
use that field.  You can define your own subscribt in working storage
and use it in that table and use ADD, SUBTRACT, MULTIPLY whatever on
it.  Again, lack of understanding of the language is not a failing of
the language.

>They did, in fact, generally improve efficiency, and provide better
>error detection. They were not a "mistake", although it seems there
…[99 more quoted lines elided]…
>

About Fortran....I once supported a teller processing system that was
written entirely in Fortran.  The source was compiled on the mainframe
and the object downloaded to the teller station control unit.  The
system built screens and processed them and communicated between the
controller which communicted with the mainframe.  Why the authors
chose Fortran instead of Assmbler is beyond me, but it did show that
Fortran had more uses than just statistics and table manipulation.

Regards,
```

```
SkippyPB wrote:

> On Wed, 23 Nov 2011 16:59:00 -0800 (PST), Richard
>
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> many, many moons.  I'd say it has paid for itself long ago.

The call of a module like
         L     R15,=A(&MODUL)      *
         BASR  R14,R15             *
and the entry code of the module
         USING *,R15
         STM   R14,R12,12(R13)     * SAVE REGISTERS. 
and the exit code of the module
         LM    R14,R12,12(R13)     *
         BR    R14
are AFAIK not more expensive than the equivalence for PERFORM.

> About Fortran....I once supported a teller processing system that was
> written entirely in Fortran.  The source was compiled on the mainframe
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Fortran had more uses than just statistics and table manipulation.

Once a user of a mail monitoring package (written in FORTRAN) noticed it
didn't run very efficiently. So we wrote a prototype of a FORTRAN
compiler in COBOL which gained them 50% of the CPU time (and the need
to buy an additional mainframe). See the example at 
http://fred.mobach.nl/download/forass.cbl.gz
```

```
On Wed, 30 Nov 2011 02:29:00 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>Robert Wessel wrote:
><snipped>>
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>I'd be interested to know.


We used an approach a number of other folks used for HLL TSR support.
Basically an (assembler) library which when called would install the
running program as a TSR, set up all the interrupt vectors that needed
to be shelled, and whatnot.  It also watched for the triggering
events, which were appropriately set from the Cobol program.

The application itself operated in a polled mode.  When it decided to
go to sleep, it would call "TSR_WAIT", and the library would return
control to the foreground program.  When a waking event occurred, it
was initially handled by the library, which finished by switching
stacks and then returning to the Cobol application from the TSR_WAIT
call.  The application could then check to see exactly what event
caused the wakeup.

The application could also check for events that occurred while it was
active.

So there was no direct interrupt handling or that sort of thing in the
Cobol program, and all of the weirdness happened outside.  With a few
exceptions (memory allocation and program invocations, for example),
this mostly worked as normal.

Additional support was in the TSR library to save and restore the
screen state, although our screen management library did most of that
already, it just needed to do it at odd times (basically if the Cobol
application wanted to hit the screen, say popping up after a hotkey,
it would call TSR_SAVE_SCREEEN, SCREENER_INIT, ...interact...,
SCREENER_CLOSE, TSR_RESTORE_SCREEN - and only the first and last of
those was actually new).  And a few other things like that

Realia's contribution was more an avoidance of bad behavior when
interfacing with DOS and the BIOS.
```

```
I share data structures very easily between subprograms with USING.

What you have seen or not is not a limitation.

If dynamically CALLed subprograms are used (as I mostly do) then after
loading the program it is added to a table so that it can be called
using a reference to the table, this is a few more instructions than
the hard coded CALL that can be compiled for a nested sub-program.
Even a statically linked non-nested CALL needs a fixup.

are simply puffery.

limitations.


> >> >> **Originally subscripts in Fortran were limited to the general form
> >> >> ((A*B) +/- C), and the obvious degenerate forms of that.  The point
…[48 more quoted lines elided]…
> array as the same expression in C.

Subscript checks are expensive and are usually on during development
when the program should be checked for all boundary conditions.
Turning them off for production is an optimisation.

It is inconsistent of you to complain about lack of optimisation and
also of subscript checks being off.


> But the point was that the Fortran guys (initially) restricted the
> forms of the allowed subscript expression in order to help
> optimization, not error checking.

FORTRAN originally (in the late 1950s) allowed subscript expression
with multiply, add and subtract. Please explain the mechanism where
this assisted optimisation ?


> >> >In any case FORTRAN only had arrays of numbers (int, real, double,
> >> >etc) and abused them by storing text strings across array elements.
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> TYPEs), in the mid seventies.

So it was hit and miss whether you could use them or not, and whether
your program would compile or not.

Checking some FORTRAN manuals that I have from the 1970s, for ICL
System4 and 1900, these do not have structures so they weren't 'soon
enough' for my use of FORTRAN.
```

- **References:** `<jaf2h4$ejf$1@reader1.panix.com> <o6lnc71uumfueqvoj440tp2925l94cpvgn@4ax.com> <9j31rcFn3rU1@mid.individual.net> <0kfpc7171lllj4j4k993p2s2kfaleuhphb@4ax.com> <733d570d-92fe-4a5f-a807-47c6eee7afce@d37g2000prg.googlegroups.com> <a6mqc796vj45gau745s2hsi33drer5oir4@4ax.com> <ad57c1af-5f8b-4878-b812-7626224d3933@s6g2000vbc.googlegroups.com> <a5p8d7h2q63rp1epto5eq3f3r2efl6hg32@4ax.com> <86ed374f-9e87-4186-b4b1-272275fdd852@ca1g2000vbb.googlegroups.com>`

```
On Mon, 28 Nov 2011 23:29:34 -0800 (PST), Richard
<riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

>On Nov 29, 6:06ï¿½pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
>> On Wed, 23 Nov 2011 16:59:00 -0800 (PST), Richard
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
>Please feel free to use whatever language that you find non-ugly.


At least with a perform, you can access the defined structure from all
the routines.  You need to redescribe the data areas with a (Cobol-85)
subprogram.


>> >>ï¿½Sure nested subprograms were better, but did you ever
>> >> see anyone write a program with no performs, just calls to nested
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
>Even a statically linked non-nested CALL needs a fixup.


Statically linked subprograms, whether nested or "conventional,"
typically use the same calling convention, perhaps plus or minus a
smidge.  Dynamically linked subprograms usually have a bit more
overhead.  All of those calling conventions are typically quite
expensive.

>> >> And while not a direct fault of
>> >> the language, by the time Cobol-85 features became available, much of
…[56 more quoted lines elided]…
>different constraints.


Very little different.  We're talking about arrays.  The Fortran guys,
who care *a lot* more about array performance (especially since array
elements are often very small - a single scalar, in the case of
classic Fortran), did not see fit to introduce an alternate mechanism
for indexing, just to improve performance.

The compilation techniques for optimizing array accesses are very,
very old.  In fact strength reduction and loop invariant hoisting,
were implemented in some of the earliest Fortran compilers.  To answer
you question below, making that easier was the motivation for the
restricted forms of subscript expressions in the original Fortran.
They quickly discovered that they didn't need those restrictions, and
eliminated them.

And if I've harped too much on Fortran, these were common
optimizations in Algol as well.


>> >> Most decent compilers are quite capable of optimizing most of the
>> >> subscripting to be equivalent of what you'd hope a index would
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>are simply puffery.


For whatever reason, with or without optimization the code emitted
from many Cobol compilers is terrible (despite vendor claims to the
contrary).  Certainly the vendors of some of the most important
compilers have rarely, if ever, had any motivation to improve them.


>> >>ï¿½My
>> >> point with the Fortran example was that if the Fortran guys (or
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
>limitations.


Yep, limitations are certainly on display here...


>> >> >> **Originally subscripts in Fortran were limited to the general form
>> >> >> ((A*B) +/- C), and the obvious degenerate forms of that. ï¿½The point
…[55 more quoted lines elided]…
>also of subscript checks being off.


Hardly.  If subscript range checks are a feature of the language, they
probably need to be implemented by the compiler.  With most Cobol
compilers set to "optimize," they're not.  So you can't claim that
(say) C is worse because it doesn't have range checks, if your running
you Cobol code without the range checks turned on.  And the notion
that you only have them on during development is silly - almost all of
the buffer overflows that happen to C programs didn't happen during
testing, just with peculiar, often malicious, input data.

While not impacting the sixties and seventies, it's now pretty clear
that you can optimize much of the work of range checks away (obviously
you need a language that preserves enough size information), so that
they add very little overhead.  For example, if you have a loop, if
you can check the range of the loop, you can often determine that
you'll never generate a bad (out of range) subscript.  Even many C
compilers have optional overflow checking (not perfect, but if turned
on, it will usually catch most buffer overflows within a short time of
them happening).


>> But the point was that the Fortran guys (initially) restricted the
>> forms of the allowed subscript expression in order to help
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>this assisted optimisation ?


See above.


>> >> >In any case FORTRAN only had arrays of numbers (int, real, double,
>> >> >etc) and abused them by storing text strings across array elements.
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>enough' for my use of FORTRAN.


The point being that the many of the guys who really cared about array
performance, implemented arrays of structures, using subscripts, a
really long time ago.  And that it's not really different if you have
larger or more varied array element sizes (which you had anyway with
multi-dimensional arrays, which, again, were far more commonly used in
Fortran than Cobol).
```

```
EXTERNAL yet ?

…[8 more quoted lines elided]…

> >different constraints.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> for indexing, just to improve performance.

Array elements of a single scalar _is_ a different constraint.

But FORTRAN _did_ introduce changes. They added structures (25 years
later) and allowed any expression to be used as a subscript.

If it is so important to you then I suggest that you continue using
FORTRAN.

> very old.  In fact strength reduction and loop invariant hoisting,
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…

'Quickly' being 25 years for FORTRAN90.

> optimizations in Algol as well.

You appear to imagine that COBOL compiler writers are unaware of
optimising. It may well be that the FORTRAN language was originally
formulated with restrictions to assist the compiler writer while COBOL
was formulated to assist the programmer.

Since then they have moved closer together. FORTRAN changed to improve
programmer flexibility while COBOL changed to improve performance.
Neither are 'faults'.
```

```
> the buffer overflows that happen to C programs didn't happen during
> testing, just with peculiar, often malicious, input data.

You seem unaware of proper development procedures and coding
techniques. Bound checks are there to demonstrate that adequate
checking has been done by the programmer, not to replace those checks.
```

```
BOUND checking is a feature (that can be turned on or off) in the current 
ANSI/ISO Standard.  It is implmented by (some, but not many) COBOL 
implmentors

Look fo the
  EC-RANGE
featgure of the Common Exception facility.

Many (most?) implentors did provide extensions for this before the Standard 
defined this.  (e.g. Micro Focus BOUNDCHECK and IBM SSRANGE).  These 
features also detect reference modificaiton out-of-bound situations (and can 
be turned on or off at run-time so minimal performance hits exist for 
"production" code with "validated" data)
```

```
On Dec 4, 9:22 pm, Clark F Morris <cfmpub...@ns.sympatico.ca> wrote:
> On Mon, 28 Nov 2011 23:06:59 -0600, Robert Wessel
>
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> general the compiler is good at optimization.

Mr Wessel's complaint was temporally anomalous! The specific
optimization technique known as "strength reduction" was
discovered circa 1977 and became generally applicable to
COBOL with the addition of in-line PERFORM in 1985. It may
also applie to the movement of code for out-of-line PERFORMs
to make them equivalent to in-line PERFORMSs. But it was NOT
available when indexing was implemented for compilers based
on the 1965 revision.

For that matter, it was NOT available for "the FORTRAN guys"
until 1977!

See < http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strength_reduction >
for a description of the technique.
```

- **From:** Robert Wessel <robertwessel2@yahoo.com>

```
If you'll read John Backus', "The History of Fortran I, II, and III"

as implemented for the first Fortran compiler.  Although he doesn't
call it that.
```

```
On Dec 5, 4:35 pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> On Mon, 5 Dec 2011 10:30:17 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
> <rs847...@gmail.com> wrote:
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
> call it that.

I, respectfully, withdraw my comments regarding FORTRAN.

>
> And you're using, as an excuse, the fact that Cobol didn't have a
> proper looping construct until '85?!  Seriously?  Have you guys ever
> heard the story of the Genius Tailor?

Apology, elsewhere, noted.

After my prior post, I did some sleuthing regarding John Cocke
whose 1977 paper is referenced in the cited wikipedia article.
See also < http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Cocke >.
=====
John Cocke (May 30, 1925 – July 16, 2002) was an American
computer scientist recognized for his large contribution to
computer architecture and optimizing compiler design. He is
considered by many to be "the father of RISC architecture."

Cocke spent his entire career as an industrial researcher
for IBM, from 1956 to 1992.
=====

Looking for Cocke in other sources, I tracked the idea of
"strength reduction" for arrays to 1972 and stopped at some
references to papers by others from 1969.

Though I could not find references to the early '60s, I am
willing to accept that indexing ("strength reduction" for
arrays) was known at that time. But, if it was a known
technique for FORTRAN arrays, then it was a known technique
for COBOL arrays. That being the case, then FORTRAN-style
indexing must have been rejected as inappropriate for COBOL.

A plausible reason for such a rejection was that application
to COBOL would likely have required global analysis of the
procedure division -- something not applicable to FORTRAN
and not easily done with "core" measured in kilobytes.
Using index-names provides the benefits of indexing without
requiring global analysis!

I still hold that the earliest FORTRAN-style indexing could
have been applied to COBOL was the 1985 standard. If the
compiler, when processing an in-line perform, can determine
that a [data-name as a] subscript will not change or be
saved during the loop, then it can apply indexing.
```

```
On Mon, 5 Dec 2011 19:06:25 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
<rs847925@gmail.com> wrote:

>On Dec 5, 4:35ï¿½pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
>> On Mon, 5 Dec 2011 10:30:17 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
…[74 more quoted lines elided]…
>saved during the loop, then it can apply indexing.


I think we could fruitlessly debate what optimization could have been
implemented for which compiler, and when, for a very long time.

Even if you applied only a fairly local analysis, many cases would be
covered as the same (or nearby) array elements are often accessed
multiple times within a paragraph, and that sort of subscript
reference could easily be optimized (although that would properly be
common subexpression elimination, rather than strength reduction).

Although I'd argue that adding a proper looping construct would have
been a far better use of everybody's time than indexes.

My complaint about Cobol indexes is less the INDEXED BY clause, and
more the absurdly separate method of manipulating them.

If you want to provide an optimization hint (I'd take a skeptical
stance about the hint being useful in the long term*, although in the
context of the early sixties, I'd certainly grant some slack), a
dedicated "INDEXED BY" variable is not unreasonable (that does miss
the important case where you want to access two tables with the same
subscript).  OTOH, reversing it makes some sense too (IOW, allow a
numeric field to say "INDEXES TABLE-1").

But it should at least be an ordinary numeric variable for all other
purposes.


*Optimization hints in languages are more notable for being cruft to
work around over time, rather than being generally useful (which is
not to say there aren't counterexamples), so making them fairly
ignorable is generally a good idea.
```

```
declarations which is prevented by declaring the index(es) as part of

There is USAGE INDEX, so separate indexes can be declared.
```

```
temp-subscript" statements around the call and then manipulate temp
```

```
On Dec 6, 9:12 pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> On Mon, 5 Dec 2011 21:40:28 -0800 (PST), Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz>
> wrote:
>

> >> Although I'd argue that adding a proper looping construct would have
> >> been a far better use of everybody's time than indexes.
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> pre-85 Cobol in this regard.

Whether it improves clarity or reduces it is a matter of opinion, and
entirely what one is used to. In 'Structured Programming' terms (eg
Jackson) taking the bodies out of line improves the clarity of the
higher level structure.

> And a named label as the end of loop is pretty ugly too, but at least
> the loop body is inline.

It would be possible to write a complete large program entirely in-
line, starting at the first line and looping and dropping through to
the last. This would not be the best way to write large programs.
Separating the program into layers improves clarity of the structure,
as well as allowing reuse. FORTRAN and COBOL do this differently.

The FORTRAN language was developed to aid the compiler writer to write
efficient machine code. The COBOL language was developed to aid the
application programmer.


> >> My complaint about Cobol indexes is less the INDEXED BY clause, and
> >> more the absurdly separate method of manipulating them.
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> regard).

You are confused, COBOL was not intended to be concise.

If you want concise then use APL.

> >So you complain that there is an "absurdly separate method" for
> >indexes and then advocate an absurdly separate method for declaring
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> to use.

Indexes do _not_ "require wholesale changes". Subscripts still work in
existing programs, there is no _requirement_ for any change. As
indexes have been around for over 40 years then programs will have
been written with one or the other or both.

You appear to be obsessed with a crusade of forcing others to change
how their programs work.

If you wish to write your own language that is slightly different from
COBOL then please do so and let anyone who wishes follow you. If you
want to use FORTRAN then you can do that too (though you may find
difficulty in finding remuneration).

It is far too late to get COBOL made to be more like FORTRAN, that was
for the 1950s.

> > Allowing a field to be declared as an index separately from
> >the table makes no sense, it would allow a physical separation of the
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> at least do that for me?

No one cares. C programmers don't swap pointers and subscript values.
If you want the subscript value in COBOL then don't use indexes, or
increment a numeric item whenever you increment the index.

You have this hate of 'SET', then don't use it.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Subscript and other optimizations was Re: 77 Levels

```
On Tue, 6 Dec 2011 10:13:35 -0800 (PST), Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>

That's  not what JSP requires at all.  Of course Jackson was trying
specifically to help Cobol programmers, so that was a natural result
of the early formulations.

>> the loop body is inline.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>as well as allowing reuse. FORTRAN and COBOL do this differently.


Where did I ever suggest doing any such thing?  (Old) Cobol certainly
nudges one towards large monolithic programs with its lack of proper
subroutines.


>The FORTRAN language was developed to aid the compiler writer to write
>efficient machine code. The COBOL language was developed to aid the
>application programmer.


Right.  So why indexes that expose a micro-optimization and make the
language more complex?


>> >> My complaint about Cobol indexes is less the INDEXED BY clause, and
>> >> more the absurdly separate method of manipulating them.
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>If you want concise then use APL.

constructs, and I pointed out that they were not, and one of the forms
allowed certain thing to be expressed concisely.

Nor is wordiness equivalent to clarity.


>> >So you complain that there is an "absurdly separate method" for
>> >indexes and then advocate an absurdly separate method for declaring
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>how their programs work.


Not a crusade, but to the extent that computing would be improved by
the use of better tools, I'd certainly encourage that.  Cobol is a bad
tool (the standard has certainly improved in recent years - it's still
not good, but rather less horrible - but that's been pretty irrelevant
to the real world).  It was an interesting experiment when we didn't
know any better, but it clearly failed in its purpose to allow
non-programmers access to code.  And it was specifically designed
without much consideration for professional programmers (see Grace
Hopper).  

But Cobol provided a number of things valuable to the data processing
(as opposed to CS) communities, scaled decimal arithmetic being a
large, and obvious example.  I've certainly (and publically*) placed a
fair bit of blame on the CS community for Cobol's extended life -
their refusal to deal with requirements like currency calculations in
"good" languages left many people without real alternatives. Defending
Cobol as a whole because it provide those things, OTOH, is silly.


*and I usually get beat up for that too.


>If you wish to write your own language that is slightly different from
>COBOL then please do so and let anyone who wishes follow you. If you
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
>for the 1950s.


I don't want to change Cobol anymore.  Cobol was an experiment of the
late 50s, and made many mistakes, some unfixable, other not.  Even
many of the fixable ones were not fixed until much, much later.  And
many of the things which were done, indexes, for example, or not done
inline loops, types, for example, are difficult to justify as having
and sort of coherency.


>> > Allowing a field to be declared as an index separately from
>> >the table makes no sense, it would allow a physical separation of the
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
>You have this hate of 'SET', then don't use it.


I don't hate it - I find it silly.  You, on the other hand, seem
excessively fond of it.
```

```
On Tue, 6 Dec 2011 15:24:02 -0800 (PST), Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
wrote:

>On Dec 7, 8:18ï¿½am, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
>> On Tue, 6 Dec 2011 10:13:35 -0800 (PST), Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz>
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
>stuff out of line and stick it somewhere else.


Modest sized loops are usually clearer if coded inline.  And the vast
majority of loops are short.


>> >> And a named label as the end of loop is pretty ugly too, but at least
>> >> the loop body is inline.
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
>to exclude COBOL CALL ?


Apparently we'll have to disagree about the near unusability of old
style Cobol CALLs for subroutines.
 

>> >The FORTRAN language was developed to aid the compiler writer to write
>> >efficient machine code. The COBOL language was developed to aid the
…[38 more quoted lines elided]…
>beefs with Cobol indexes - they help nothing in that regard)."


My position, which should be obvious, even if you disagree with it, is
that Cobol indexes actually are negatives in terms of expressing a
problem, and fairly clearly not necessary at all, even for performance
reasons.  And indexes add no expressiveness.  Contrast, for example,
SEARCH ALL, which implements a slightly-tricky-to-get-right algorithm
in a very concise way (albeit still rather wordy from the perspective
of most languages).


>> Nor is wordiness equivalent to clarity.
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>that simply through lack of usage and familiarity ?

By that standard, we'd never have invented HLLs at all!

But one more time.  Using indexes, leads to more code (statements,
words, typing, reading) to express the same concept, while adding a
number of non-trivial rules that need to obeyed regarding their use.
While adding nothing to you're ability to express the problem.  This
is unlikely to lead to clarity.


>> >> >So you complain that there is an "absurdly separate method" for
>> >> >indexes and then advocate an absurdly separate method for declaring
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
>in the 60s.


And just what relevance do the Cobol standards after '85 have in the
real world?


>> but it clearly failed in its purpose to allow
>> non-programmers access to code.
>
>It was far better at this than any other contemporary language.


Sure.  Cobol got 2% of the way to that goal, and no other contemporary
general purpose language got past .1%.  So it failed utterly at that
goal while trouncing its competitors.


>> And it was specifically designed
>> without much consideration for professional programmers (see Grace
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>doing for performance reasons.


I was quoting (summarized, at least) Grace Hopper, for crying out
loud...


>> But Cobol provided a number of things valuable to the data processing
>> (as opposed to CS) communities, scaled decimal arithmetic being a
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>I'm not surprised.


This becomes tedious...

A major reason Cobol was heavily used was good support of certain
functions near-and-dear to business programmers.  Languages I'd argue
are clearly better (Algol, for example), lacked some of those
features, and were often dismissed by the DP community as unsuitable
for their use, with some justification.  The CS community, OTOH,
largely refused to their pretty little languages with nasty little
things like scaled decimal arithmetic.  The current attitude of
developers of Java, C#, and similar is markedly different.  Instead
much of the CS community looked down their noses at the DP community,
said things like "the use of Cobol cripples the mind," and continued
to ignore their needs.   The DP community largely returned the favor
and attitude, and largely dismissed "all that ivory tower stuff."


>> >If you wish to write your own language that is slightly different from
>> >COBOL then please do so and let anyone who wishes follow you. If you
…[54 more quoted lines elided]…
>them.
```

```
Not sure why I'm bothering, but...  How is that indexes make it harder to express a problem?

Here are some code snippets I just put in to production a few weeks ago:

 01  wire-table.                                                 
     05  wire-tag-cnt            pic 999.                        
     05  wire-tag-entry          occurs 100 times                
                                        depending on wire-tag-cnt
                                        ascending key wte-tag    
                                        indexed by tagidx.       
         10.                                                     
             15  wte-tag         pic x(4).                       
             15  wte-tag-name    pic x(50).                      
             15  wte-elem-cnt    pic 99.                         
         10                      occurs 30 times                 
                                        indexed by elemidx.      
             15  wte-element-nbr pic 99.                         
             15  wte-element-name pic x(50).                     
             15  wte-element-show-name pic x.                    
             15  wte-element-cust-show pic x.                    
             15  wte-element-code pic x(4).                      
             15  wte-type        pic x.                          

 01  value-text.                                             
     05  pic x occurs 1 to 15000 depending on value-text-len.
 77  value-text-len              pic 9(7) comp.              

 linkage section.
 77  fedwire-len                 pic s9(9) comp.          
 01  fedwire-buffer.                                      
     05  pic x occurs 1 to 20000 depending on fedwire-len.
 copy wireinfo.                                           

 procedure division using fedwire-len fedwire-buffer wireinfo.  
     ...   

 process-elements.                          
     search all wire-tag-entry              
     at end                                 
         display 'tag not found: ' wire-tag 
     when wte-tag (tagidx) = wire-tag       
         perform handle-elements            
     end-search                             
     .                                      
                                            
 handle-elements.            
     move 0 to value-text-len
     set elemidx to 1        
     perform parse-elements  
     .                       
                             
 parse-elements.                                            
     move space to delim-1                           
     set process-element to true                     
     perform parse-element                           
             until wte-type (tagidx elemidx) = spaces
                or elemidx > max-elem-per-tag        
                or delim-1 = '{'                     
                or wirebuf-pos > fedwire-len         
     .                                               
 parse-element.                                            
     move wte-len (tagidx elemidx) to value-text-len
     move space to delim-1                                        
     evaluate wte-type (tagidx elemidx)                           
     when 'F'                                                     
         unstring fedwire-buffer into value-text                  
                  pointer wirebuf-pos                             
     when 'V'                                                     
         unstring fedwire-buffer delimited by '*' or '{'          
                  into value-text delimiter in delim-1            
                  pointer wirebuf-pos                             
     when '4'                                                     
         unstring fedwire-buffer into hold-len pointer wirebuf-pos
         move hold-len to value-text-len                          
         unstring fedwire-buffer into value-text
                  pointer wirebuf-pos                             
     when other                                                   
         display 'invalid type in parse-element: '                
                 wte-type (tagidx elemidx)                        
         call fb-abend                                                 
     end-evaluate
     move wte-element-nbr(tagidx elemidx) to wire-tag-elem
     perform write-to-wireinfo                        
     set elemidx up by 1                                  
     .                                                    

  write-to-wireinfo.                                
 *    evaluate wire-tag also wire-tag-elem-nbr      
      evaluate wire-tag also elemidx                
      when '1100' also 01                           
          move value-text to wi-format-version (1:) 
      when '1100' also 02                           
          move value-text to wi-test-prod-code    
      ...
      when '9000' also any                       
          move value-text to wi-ff-line (ffidx)  
          set ffidx up by 1                      
      end-evaluate                               
      move zero to value-text-len                        
      .

Also demonstrates some usefulness of occurs depending on.

The one thing that did annoy me is that I had to initialize some indexes like this:
set ibkidx bbkidx bnfidx drwidx orgidx orgfidx obkidx insidx
    obiidx rbkiidx drwaidx ibkiidx ibkaidx bbkiidx bbkaidx  
    bnfiidx bnfaidx bbiidx ffidx                            
    to 1                                                    

Yikes!

And yes, in 2011 I am using level 77 items!

Frank
```

```
Frank Swarbrick wrote:
> Not sure why I'm bothering, but...  How is that indexes make it
> harder to express a problem?

See "Yikes!" below.

You answered your own question :-)

(Actually, I use indexes for all table reference in COBOL, but I think 
Robert has some good points.)

>
> Here are some code snippets I just put in to production a few weeks
…[112 more quoted lines elided]…
> Frank

Frank, I'm not going to comment on the code (other than to agree with 
"Yikes!"), but I did want to say it is courageous to post code here. :-)

For myself, if faced with this problem, I would choose a language that 
easily supports Regular Expressions (COBOL does so clumsily, and it is 
platform dependent), but I accept you wanted to demonstrate use of some 
constructs which have been the subject of debate here and also that COBOL is 
the tool you have.

It comes down to a personal choice in the end (or a choice imposed by the 
COI).

Despite all the discussion and debate regarding indexes and optimization, 
unless somebody implements the improved standard, it is just bubbles.

Robert observed that the last credible standard was the 85 one and I agree 
with him. Neverthless, a huge amount of work went into the 2002 COBOL 
standard and it is a pity to see it dismissed as not useful by most vendors. 
(Some vendors have implemented quite a bit of it but, as far as I know, 
nobody has implemented all of it. Meanwhile, COBOL has lost credibility and 
the world is voting with its feet.) You can argue all you like about 77 
levels, OCCURS...DEPENDING ON, indexes versus subscripts, or any other 
aspect of Ye Olde COBOL but if Ye Olde COBOL has no relevance then your 
argument is like hoar frost in sunshine. Most COBOL users are stuck with 
COBOL 85 for whatever platform they are using. That makes it hard to argue a 
case for continued use of COBOL. Why would you pay money to be tied to a 27 
year old standard that doesn't play nicely with modern platforms and 
environments (unless you implement the OO form of it, and most practitioners 
dismiss OOP as unnecessary - usually because their opinion about that is 
uninformed...) when you can simply let go of it and get something better for 
free?

Please note I have not contributed to the latter part of this thread (which 
is now becoming tiresome, at least, it is to me... I respect the right of 
the posters to continue ad infinitum... :-)) as I see no point in it.

What minds will be changed? Will COBOL be improved? Hey! the sun is 
shining... I'm off to the beach. :-)

Pete.
```

- **Date:** 2011-12-08T01:06:33-06:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1fn0e75i92oleo78eq0aqh2ucj0k7s0mkt@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<9c5rd7h5cdfulhophs54gk696obba9irap@4ax.com> <ba37a54e-babb-4148-86a8-4a488c1fa485@b32g2000yqn.googlegroups.com> <vhird711lp19rrt3r5ef2l79o482fh9oum@4ax.com> <7be752cb-066e-4f49-9cbe-57bc8d3872e6@g7g2000vbd.googlegroups.com> <0iosd791bcce4373alcmbhngsmnidml0ja@4ax.com> <adf50cbd-e191-4e20-8246-8c8e99d7ee22@i6g2000vbe.googlegroups.com> <up2ud79tkmmae0cd8os0bfcme9rvl1rr8s@4ax.com> <11305782.365.1323276498995.JavaMail.geo-discussion-forums@prcx11>`

```
On Wed, 7 Dec 2011 08:48:18 -0800 (PST), Frank Swarbrick
<fswarbrick@gmail.com> wrote:

>Not sure why I'm bothering, but...  How is that indexes make it harder to express a problem?
>
…[109 more quoted lines elided]…
>And yes, in 2011 I am using level 77 items!


In terms of expressiveness, you could have done the above (and
honestly, I've not taken the time to look at it too closely), just as
easily with subscripts.  Ignoring for a moment that SEARCH (ALL)
requires the use of a subscript.  Other than changing a few SETs to
ADDs or MOVEs, your code would be unchanged.  Your code snippet also
seems to avoid any of the places where indexes are awkward.

By adding a *second* way to specify subscripts, you (1) have to decide
to use (or not) indexes, (2) use different, and more limited
statements to manipulate indexes, and (3) deal with additional
restrictions in how you can actually use an index.  And while those
are not huge items, they are necessarily more complex than just using
subscripts.  For that added complexity, you get *no* improvement in
expressiveness, just additional work.

Where I started this thread was all that then remains is a performance
improvement, and the reality of that is more than a little
questionable (hence all the talk of strength reduction in 1958 Fortran
compilers).  And even if not, in the context of 50 year old compilers,
a conceptually much simpler implementation of indexes - both from the
programmer's and compiler writer's perspective - was trivial.  And if
even that isn't there (IOW, there are essentially no benefits, yet
there are downsides), aren't indexes a mistake?
```

```
Robert Wessel wrote:
> On Wed, 7 Dec 2011 08:48:18 -0800 (PST), Frank Swarbrick
> <fswarbrick@gmail.com> wrote:
…[140 more quoted lines elided]…
> there are downsides), aren't indexes a mistake?

No, not a mistake; maybe a not-totally-necessary alternative.

I've folllowed your arguments, Robert, and, although I always use indexes 
for table reference in COBOL, I kept an open mind.

IF the optimization had been implemented so there was no actual need for an 
INDEX type, you might have a case. But it wasn't, so, at least in my 
opinion, you don't.

The reality is that people have a choice between subscripting and/or 
indexing, just as they have a choice about writing "add 1 to count" or 
"compute count = count + 1".

I can't see a downside to having options... :-)

Pete.
```

```
On Thu, 8 Dec 2011 22:57:39 +1300, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>Robert Wessel wrote:
>> On Wed, 7 Dec 2011 08:48:18 -0800 (PST), Frank Swarbrick
…[156 more quoted lines elided]…
>I can't see a downside to having options... :-)


Only if they provide real choices, or present at some real advantages.
"Add 1 to count" (or the C "+=" operator, for example), provides a
rather more concise, and clearer, way of altering a variable in a
simple way - certainly clearer than the compute - and shorter and less
error prone to boot.  I don't, however, expect different code to be
generated for the two statements.  OTOH, a complex expression is *not*
going to be clearer if expressed as a long sequence of ADDs,
MULTIPLYs, etc., instead of a COMPUTE.  But "SET index UP BY 1"
appears to offer no advantages over an ADD.

But the question remains - why was the optimization not implemented? I
think we've established that contemporaneous compilers for other
languages did do optimizations similar to that.  "Cobol is special"
might well be the actual reason, but it feels a bit thin.

And if an optimization hint was necessary for the compiler technology
of the era, then why not simply let us treat it is a numeric value as
it basically is, and allow us to write "ADD 1 TO index" as well?  Or
"COMPUTE index2 = index1 + 3"?  After all, you've always been allowed
to write "IF index > numeric-field", so it's not like the compiler was
ever unable to do the conversion from index to subscript automatically
anyway.   At which point most of my objection goes away.

So (1) I'm not convinced there was an actual performance reason to do
indexes, and (2) if there was, they were implemented badly.

Stubborn cuss, aren't I?
```

```
On Dec 8, 11:35 am, Frank Swarbrick <fswarbr...@gmail.com> wrote:
> What are others?  The only other one I've found is I cannot "DISPLAY" them (DISPLAY MYIDX) during debugging.

I can't know what you may have tried, but index values may
be displayed indirectly. YMMV
=====
       identification division.
       program-id. idx-dbg.
       data division.
       working-storage section.
       77 dbg-index index.
       77 dbg-comp redefines dbg-index comp pic 9(8).
       1 a-table.
        2 occurs 100000 indexed by te-idx.
         3 table-entry pic x(40).
       procedure division.
       begin.
           set te-idx to 1
           set dbg-index to te-idx
           display dbg-comp
           set te-idx to 100000
           set dbg-index to te-idx
           display dbg-comp
           set te-idx to 50001
           set dbg-index to te-idx
           display dbg-comp
           stop run
           .
=====
00000000
03999960
02000000
=====
```

```
in any case the argument has been irrelevant for decades. Get over it,
```

```
While I've decided to stop discussing this with you, I do want to
agree that I misremembered my quote.  It was not Hopper, rather Jean
Sammet, another member of the CODASYL committee which developed Cobol,
who said:

"The users for who COBOL was designed were actually two subclasses of
those people concerned with business data processing problems. One is
the relatively inexperienced programmer for whom the naturalness of
COBOL would be an asset, while the other type of user would be
essentially anybody who had not written the program initially. In
other words, the readability of COBOL programs would provide
documentation to all who might wish to examine the programs, including
the supervisory or management personnel. Little attempt was made to
cater for professional programmers."
```

```
On Dec 8, 8:06 pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> On Wed, 7 Dec 2011 10:10:19 -0800 (PST), Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz>
> wrote:
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> While I've decided to stop discussing this with you,

That is a pity because I was hoping that you would eventually get
around to attempting to substantiate your assertion that COBOL CALLs
were not "proper" and were "unusable". But I wasn't holding my breath.


> I do want to
> agree that I misremembered my quote.  It was not Hopper, rather Jean
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> cater for professional programmers."

You seem to misunderstand the context of that comment. In the 1950s,
when COBOL was being designed, programmers were expected to rewire
computers, structure their programs around the timings of mercury tube
memory, be able code directly in binary, and debug the hardware.

Those are the people who were referred to in the 1950s as
'professional programmers'. They were not interested in mere business
applications (except the LEO team of course). A language designed for
them would turn out to be like ACL (Atlas Commercial Language) or BCPL
(Basic Combined Programming Language). The COBOL designers were not
interested in converting those into high-level language programmers.

COBOL certainly was intended for people who had experience in business
(rather than electronics) and knew a packing slip from a credit note,
as well as 'inexperienced' (ie third generation) programmers. In the
60s these became professional programmers too, just on different types
of applications.

As counter examples BASIC and Algol-68W (which became Pascal) were
intended purely as teaching languages and would not be used
professionally.

There is (or was in the late 1950s) a distinction between
'professional programmers' and those who programmed professionally (as
distinct from amateurs such as civil engineers writing FORTRAN).


> >> >Dealing with currency is not a CS problem, it _is_ a 'professional
> >> >programmer' problem.
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> Cobol long ago?

They certainly moved on from complaining about what happened in the
60s. You haven't though.
```

```
On Dec 7, 8:18 am, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> On Tue, 6 Dec 2011 10:13:35 -0800 (PST), Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz>

> >> Well, given that nobody else has chosen to do it the old Cobol way,

…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> Where did I ever suggest doing any such thing?

You were advocating that inline code aided clarity, or specifically
that taking blocks out-of-line was a "bad idea in terms of clarity".

The ultimate expression of this is the program that is entirely in-
line, which is thus entirely monolithic.

If you learned programming in FORTRAN then you may well think that in-
line is 'better' because that is the only way to do it. COBOL
programmers may well think that out-of line is perfectly clear given
adequate paragraph naming.

It may well be that a high level structure as:

          PERFORM Open-Product-File
          PERFORM Process-Product-Records
              UNTIL End-Of-Product-File
                 OR Fatal-Error
          PERFORM Close-Product-File

is perfectly clear as to what is intended while putting all the
processing code in-line (and thus without the paragraph names) would
be considerably less clear.

'Clarity' is entirely what one is used to.


> (Old) Cobol certainly
> nudges one towards large monolithic programs with its lack of proper
> subroutines.

There is no lack of 'proper subroutines'.
```

```
On Dec 5, 11:37 pm, Robert Wessel <robertwess...@yahoo.com> wrote:
> On Mon, 5 Dec 2011 19:06:25 -0800 (PST), Rick Smith
> <rs847...@gmail.com> wrote:

[snip]

> >Though I could not find references to the early '60s, I am
> >willing to accept that indexing ("strength reduction" for
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
> been a far better use of everybody's time than indexes.

What is now considered "proper" came wih structured programming,
in the early '70s. Indexing was circa 1965.

> My complaint about Cobol indexes is less the INDEXED BY clause, and
> more the absurdly separate method of manipulating them.

[Data-names as] subscripts belong to class numeric. [Index-names
as] subscripts belong to class index. Different classes. Different
operators. Quite reasonable!

> If you want to provide an optimization hint (I'd take a skeptical
> stance about the hint being useful in the long term*, although in the
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> subscript).

This gets back to the "size of a table entry" issue. Unless the size
of entries in the two tables is the same, stepping through the tables
will occur at diffent rates. Some compilers have pemitted the same
index-name to be used with two different table, when the size of
entries is the same; it is not permitted by the standard.

>  OTOH, reversing it makes some sense too (IOW, allow a
> numeric field to say "INDEXES TABLE-1").

> But it should at least be an ordinary numeric variable for all other
> purposes.

This is a conflict between numeric and index classes and their
distinct limitations. Consider that 'move 0 to numeric-field'
is invalid as class index, but valid as class numeric. While
'set index-name to 0' IS a syntax error. Your suggestion means
that at some later point the compiler will have to note that
the 'move 0 ...' WAS a syntax error.
```
